	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Northern bald ibis</h1>
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_Bald_Ibis&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Bald Ibis">Northern Bald Ibis</a>)</span></div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_bald_ibis_(Geronticus_eremita).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Northern_bald_ibis_%28Geronticus_eremita%29.jpg/220px-Northern_bald_ibis_%28Geronticus_eremita%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Northern_bald_ibis_%28Geronticus_eremita%29.jpg/330px-Northern_bald_ibis_%28Geronticus_eremita%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Northern_bald_ibis_%28Geronticus_eremita%29.jpg/440px-Northern_bald_ibis_%28Geronticus_eremita%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2250" data-file-height="3375" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_bald_ibis_(Geronticus_eremita).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>wild in <a href="/wiki/Souss-Massa_National_Park" title="Souss-Massa National Park">Souss-Massa National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <b>northern bald ibis</b>, <b>hermit ibis</b>, or <b>waldrapp</b> (<i>Geronticus eremita</i>) is a migratory bird found in barren, semi-desert or rocky habitats, often close to running water. This 70–80&#160;cm (28–31&#160;in) glossy black <a href="/wiki/Ibis" title="Ibis">ibis</a>, which, unlike many members of the ibis family, is non-wading, has an unfeathered red face and head, and a long, curved red bill. It breeds colonially on coastal or mountain cliff ledges, where it typically lays two to three eggs in a stick nest, and feeds on lizards, insects, and other small animals.
</p><p>The northern bald ibis was once widespread across the Middle East, northern Africa, southern and central Europe, with a fossil record dating back at least 1.8&#160;million years. It disappeared from Europe over 300 years ago, although reintroduction programs in the region are underway. In 2019 there were about 700 wild birds remaining in southern <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, and fewer than 10 in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, where it was rediscovered in 2002 but where their number declined in the following years, maybe to zero.
</p><p>To combat these low numbers, <a href="/wiki/Reintroduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Reintroduction">reintroduction</a> programs have been instituted internationally in recent times, with a semi-wild breeding colony in Turkey which counted almost 250 birds in 2018<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> as well as sites in Austria, Italy, Spain, and northern Morocco. These programs and the natural growth in Morocco from about 200 birds in the 1990s helped to downlist the northern bald ibis from <a href="/wiki/Critically_endangered" title="Critically endangered">Critically Endangered</a> to <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">Endangered</a> on the <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">IUCN Red List</a> in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> There are about 2000 northern bald ibises living in captivity.
</p><p>The reasons for the species' long-term decline are unclear, but hunting, loss of foraging habitat, and <a href="/wiki/Pesticide_poisoning" title="Pesticide poisoning">pesticide poisoning</a> have been implicated in the rapid loss of colonies in recent decades.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_bald_ibis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The ibises are gregarious, long-legged wading birds with long down-curved bills. Along with the <a href="/wiki/Spoonbill" title="Spoonbill">spoonbills</a> they form one subfamily within the family <a href="/wiki/Threskiornithidae" title="Threskiornithidae">Threskiornithidae</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The northern bald ibis' closest relative, and the only other member of the genus, is the <a href="/wiki/Southern_bald_ibis" title="Southern bald ibis">southern bald ibis</a>, <i>G. calvus</i>, of southern Africa. The two <i>Geronticus</i> species differ from other ibises in that they have unfeathered faces and heads, breed on cliffs rather than in trees, and prefer arid habitats to the wetlands used by their relatives.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SASOL_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SASOL-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nbibis0371crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Nbibis0371crop.jpg/220px-Nbibis0371crop.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Nbibis0371crop.jpg/330px-Nbibis0371crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Nbibis0371crop.jpg/440px-Nbibis0371crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="519" data-file-height="417" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nbibis0371crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Conrad_Gessner" title="Conrad Gessner">Gesner's</a> 1555 <a href="/wiki/Woodcut" title="Woodcut">woodcut</a> of the northern bald ibis<sup id="cite_ref-woodcut_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-woodcut-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The northern bald ibis was described and illustrated by Swiss naturalist <a href="/wiki/Conrad_Gesner" class="mw-redirect" title="Conrad Gesner">Conrad Gesner</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Historiae_animalium_(Gesner)" class="mw-redirect" title="Historiae animalium (Gesner)">Historiae animalium</a></i> in 1555,<sup id="cite_ref-Gesner_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gesner-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Yearbook09_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yearbook09-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> and given the <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">binomial name</a> <i>Upupa eremita</i> by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in his 1758 <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-linn_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-linn-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> It was moved to its current genus by the German <a href="/wiki/Herpetologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Herpetologist">herpetologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Johann_Georg_Wagler" title="Johann Georg Wagler">Johann Georg Wagler</a> in 1832.<sup id="cite_ref-wagler_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wagler-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> This species has an interesting history of description, oblivion and rediscovery.<sup id="cite_ref-history_confusion_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history_confusion-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The species probably split into two distinct populations at least 400 years ago and, since then, the two populations have been diverging <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphologically</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecologically</a>, and genetically;<sup id="cite_ref-serraabd_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-serraabd-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> nevertheless, the Turkish and Moroccan populations of this ibis are not currently classed as separate subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> One consistent difference between the eastern and western birds is a single mutation in the <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_b" title="Cytochrome b">cytochrome b</a> <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">gene</a> of their <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pegoraro_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pegoraro-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Fossils of the northern bald ibis have been found at a <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> (c. 10,000 years ago) site in southern France,<sup id="cite_ref-cecile_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cecile-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> in middle <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> (c. 900,000 years ago) strata in Sicily,<sup id="cite_ref-boev2_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boev2-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">Pliocene</a>-Pleistocene boundary (c. 1.8&#160;million years ago) deposits on the Mediterranean coast of Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-marco_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-marco-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> What appears to be an ancestral form, <i>Geronticus balcanicus</i>, was found in the late Pliocene of Bulgaria,<sup id="cite_ref-Boev_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boev-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> further illustrating the early widespread presence of this genus in Europe, and suggesting that <i>Geronticus eremita</i> may have originated in southeastern Europe or the Middle East.<sup id="cite_ref-boev2_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boev2-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The genus name, <i>Geronticus</i>, is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek (to 1453) language text">γέρων</span>, meaning <i>old man</i> and refers to the bald head of the aged. <i>Eremita</i> is <a href="/wiki/Late_Latin" title="Late Latin">Late Latin</a> for <i>hermit</i>, from the Greek <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek (to 1453) language text">ἐρημία</span>, meaning <i>desert</i>, and refers to the arid habitats inhabited by this species. The alternative common name <i>waldrapp</i> is German for <i>forest raven</i>, the equivalent of the Latin <i>Corvo sylvatico</i> of Gesner,<sup id="cite_ref-Gesner_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gesner-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> adapted as <i>Corvus sylvaticus</i> by Linnaeus.<sup id="cite_ref-linn_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-linn-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-brookes_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brookes-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_bald_ibis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Geronticus_eremita_close_up_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Geronticus_eremita_close_up_1.jpg/220px-Geronticus_eremita_close_up_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Geronticus_eremita_close_up_1.jpg/330px-Geronticus_eremita_close_up_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Geronticus_eremita_close_up_1.jpg/440px-Geronticus_eremita_close_up_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Geronticus_eremita_close_up_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Close-up of an adult's head</div></div></div>
<p>The northern bald ibis is a large, glossy black bird, 70–80&#160;cm (28–31&#160;in) long with a 125–135&#160;cm (49–53&#160;in) wingspan and an average weight of 1.0–1.3&#160;kg (35–46&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The plumage is black, with bronze-green and violet iridescence, and there is a wispy ruff on the bird's hind neck. The face and head are dull red and unfeathered, and the long, curved bill and the legs are red.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> In flight, this bird has powerful, shallow, and flexible wing beats. It gives guttural <i>hrump</i> and high, hoarse <i>hyoh</i> calls at its breeding colonies, but is otherwise silent.<sup id="cite_ref-Mullarney_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mullarney-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The sexes are similar in plumage, although males are generally larger than females,<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and, as with other ibises that breed in colonies, have longer <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">bills</a>. The longer-billed males are more successful in attracting a mate.<sup id="cite_ref-Babbitt_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Babbitt-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The downy chick has uniformly pale brown plumage, and the fledged juvenile resembles the adult except that it has a dark head, light grey legs, and a pale bill. The unfeathered areas of the young bird's head and neck gradually become red as it matures.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Moroccan birds have a significantly longer bill than Turkish birds of the same sex.<sup id="cite_ref-Siegfried_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siegfried-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table style="width:65%;" class="wikitable">

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>If the eastern and western populations are considered to be separable subspecies, it is unclear which should be considered to be the <a href="/wiki/Subspecies#Nomenclature" title="Subspecies">nominate (first-named) form</a>, since the first description of this species was based on a now-extinct population from Switzerland which is of unknown race.<sup id="cite_ref-Gesner_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gesner-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The northern bald ibis is readily distinguished from its close relative, the southern bald ibis of Southern Africa, by the southern species' whitish face.<sup id="cite_ref-SASOL_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SASOL-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The northern bald can also be confused with the similarly dark-plumaged <a href="/wiki/Glossy_ibis" title="Glossy ibis">glossy ibis</a>, which overlaps its range, but it is larger and stockier than that species. In flight, when the bill and face colouration may not be visible, the bald ibis' less rounded wings and shorter neck give it a different profile from glossy ibis,<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and its relatively short legs mean that its feet do not project beyond the tail, unlike those of the glossy ibis.<sup id="cite_ref-Mullarney_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mullarney-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat_and_range">Habitat and range</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_bald_ibis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Habitat and range">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nbibis013.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Nbibis013.jpg/220px-Nbibis013.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Nbibis013.jpg/330px-Nbibis013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9a/Nbibis013.jpg 2x" data-file-width="440" data-file-height="404" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nbibis013.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Part of a flock in the Souss Massa stronghold</div></div></div>
<p>Unlike many other ibises, which nest in trees and feed in wetlands, the northern bald ibis breeds on undisturbed cliff ledges, and forages for food in irregularly cultivated, grazed dry areas such as semi-arid <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppes</a>, and fallow fields. The close proximity of adequate steppe feeding areas to breeding cliffs is an important habitat requirement.<sup id="cite_ref-rspb2_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rspb2-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The northern bald ibis was once widespread across the Middle East, northern Africa, and southern and central Europe;<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> fossil bones have been found at <a href="/wiki/Solothurn" title="Solothurn">Solothurn</a> dated to the Mesolithic and Neolithic Periods.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> It bred along the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhone" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhone">Rhone</a> Rivers, and in the mountains of Spain, Italy, Germany, Austria and Switzerland (Gesner's original description was of a Swiss bird),<sup id="cite_ref-Gesner2_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gesner2-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> and most probably also in the Upper <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic</a> region.<sup id="cite_ref-PT2001_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PT2001-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>  It used castle battlements as well as cliff ledges for nesting before vanishing from Europe at least three centuries ago.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> It is also extinct over most of its former range, and now almost the entirety of the wild breeding population of just over 500 birds is in Morocco, at <a href="/wiki/Souss-Massa_National_Park" title="Souss-Massa National Park">Souss-Massa National Park</a>, where there are three documented colonies, and near the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Oued_Tamri" title="Oued Tamri">Oued Tamri</a> (north of <a href="/wiki/Agadir" title="Agadir">Agadir</a>), where there is a single colony containing almost half the Moroccan breeding population. There is some movement of birds between these two sites.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Religious traditions helped this species to survive in one Turkish colony long after the species had disappeared from Europe, since it was believed that the ibis <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migrated</a> each year to guide <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a> pilgrims to <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>. The ibis was protected by its religious significance, and a festival was held annually to celebrate its return north.<sup id="cite_ref-aewa6_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aewa6-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The Turkish ibis population was centred near the small town of <a href="/wiki/Birecik" title="Birecik">Birecik</a> in the southeast of the country, and during the first half of the 20th century, the Birecik colony maintained a relatively stable population of about 500 breeding pairs, reaching an estimated total population of about 3,000 around 1930. By the 1970s, numbers had drastically declined and a captive breeding program was initiated in 1977 with one adult pair and nine chicks taken from the wild. This program largely failed to revert the decline; there were 400 birds in 1982, five pairs in 1986, and seven pairs in 1987. Only three birds returned from their wintering grounds in 1989, and just one in 1990. The returning birds died before they could reproduce, thus rendering the species extinct in the wild in Turkey as of 1992. Once the wild Turkish population became non-viable, the colony was maintained as a flock which was free-flying for most of the year but caged in autumn to prevent migration.<sup id="cite_ref-kelaynak_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kelaynak-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ibismap_2003.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ibismap_2003.png/220px-Ibismap_2003.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ibismap_2003.png/330px-Ibismap_2003.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ibismap_2003.png/440px-Ibismap_2003.png 2x" data-file-width="664" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ibismap_2003.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><span style="color:red;"><b>—</b></span>Tagged Syrian breeders migrated south through Yemen in 2006 and returned via Eritrea.<sup id="cite_ref-rspb_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rspb-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><br /><span style="color:green;"><b>—</b></span>Birds from Birecik visited the Syrian colony at Palmyra.<sup id="cite_ref-dead_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dead-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>After the demise of the migratory Turkish colony, the northern bald ibis was known to survive in the wild only at the Moroccan sites, although occasional sightings of birds in Yemen, Eritrea, Saudi Arabia, and Israel during the 1980s and 1990s suggested that there was still a colony somewhere in the Middle East.<sup id="cite_ref-Schulz_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schulz-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Intensive field surveys in spring 2002, based on the knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> nomads and local hunters, revealed that the species had never become completely extinct on the Syrian desert steppes. Following systematic searches, 15 old nesting sites were found, one, near <a href="/wiki/Palmyra" title="Palmyra">Palmyra</a>, was still hosting an active breeding colony of seven individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-serraabd_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-serraabd-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Serra2003_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serra2003-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-murdoch_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-murdoch-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Although the ibis had been declared extinct in Syria more than 70 years earlier,<sup id="cite_ref-safriel_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-safriel-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> the bird appears to have been relatively common in the desert areas until 20 years ago, when a combination of <a href="/wiki/Overexploitation" title="Overexploitation">overexploitation</a> of its range lands and increasing hunting pressures initiated a dramatic decline.<sup id="cite_ref-Serra2003_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serra2003-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-murdoch_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-murdoch-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Moroccan breeding birds are resident, dispersing along the coast after the nesting season. It has been suggested that coastal fog provides extra moisture for this population, and enables the ibises to remain year-round.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> In the rest of its former range, away from the Moroccan coastal locations, the northern bald ibis migrated south for the winter, and formerly occurred as a vagrant to Spain, Iraq, Egypt, the <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a>, and Cape Verde.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Geronticus_eremita_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.60.1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Geronticus_eremita_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.60.1.jpg/220px-Geronticus_eremita_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.60.1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Geronticus_eremita_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.60.1.jpg/330px-Geronticus_eremita_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.60.1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Geronticus_eremita_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.60.1.jpg/440px-Geronticus_eremita_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.60.1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3852" data-file-height="2482" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Geronticus_eremita_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.60.1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Geronticus eremita</i> - <a href="/wiki/MHNT" class="mw-redirect" title="MHNT">MHNT</a></div></div></div>
<p>Satellite tagging of 13 Syrian birds in 2006 showed that the three adults in the group, plus a fourth untagged adult, wintered together from February to July in the highlands of Ethiopia, where the species had not been recorded for nearly 30 years. They travelled south on the eastern side of the Red Sea via Saudi Arabia and Yemen, and returned north through Sudan and Eritrea.<sup id="cite_ref-rspb_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rspb-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-winter_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-winter-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_bald_ibis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_bald_ibis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Geronticus_eremita_Waldrapp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Geronticus_eremita_Waldrapp.jpg/220px-Geronticus_eremita_Waldrapp.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Geronticus_eremita_Waldrapp.jpg/330px-Geronticus_eremita_Waldrapp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Geronticus_eremita_Waldrapp.jpg/440px-Geronticus_eremita_Waldrapp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Geronticus_eremita_Waldrapp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A young bird</div></div></div>
<p>The northern bald ibis breeds in loosely spaced colonies, nesting on cliff ledges or amongst boulders on steep slopes, usually on the coast or near a river.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_5-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Volunteer climbers have created extra ledge spaces in the <a href="/wiki/Souss-Massa_National_Park" title="Souss-Massa National Park">Souss-Massa</a> colonies to ensure that breeding population is not limited by the availability of nest ledges,<sup id="cite_ref-rspb2_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rspb2-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and artificial <a href="/wiki/Nest_box" title="Nest box">nest boxes</a> are used in the managed colony at <a href="/wiki/Birecik" title="Birecik">Birecik</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Birecik_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birecik-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In the past, the birds also nested in buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_5-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This ibis starts breeding at three to five years of age,<sup id="cite_ref-bci_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bci-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> and pairs for life.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_5-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The male chooses a nest site, cleans it, and then advertises for a female by waving his crest and giving low rumbling calls. Once the birds have paired, the bond is reinforced through bowing displays and mutual <a href="/wiki/Preening_(bird)" title="Preening (bird)">preening</a>. The nest is a loose construction of twigs lined with grass or straw.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_5-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> <i>G. eremita</i> normally lays two to four rough-surfaced eggs, which weigh an average of 50.16&#160;g (1.769&#160;oz),<sup id="cite_ref-egg_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-egg-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and are initially blue-white with brown spots, becoming brown during <a href="/wiki/Avian_incubation" class="mw-redirect" title="Avian incubation">incubation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_5-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> An egg in the collection of the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a> was marked more thickly at the broad end, with "spots and very small blotches of yellowish brown and pale rufous". It was 0.93&#160;in long and 0.68&#160;in&#160;wide (2.37&#160;×&#160;1.73&#160;cm).<sup id="cite_ref-BrMusCat_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BrMusCat-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The clutch is incubated for 24–25 days to hatching, the chicks <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">fledge</a> in another 40–50 days,<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_5-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and the first flight takes place at about two months.<sup id="cite_ref-beaky_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beaky-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Both parents incubate and feed the chicks.<sup id="cite_ref-aewa_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aewa-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The northern bald ibis lives for an average of 20 to 25 years in captivity (oldest recorded male 37 years, oldest recorded female 30 years).<sup id="cite_ref-eep_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eep-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The average age in the wild has been estimated as 10 to 15 years.<sup id="cite_ref-bci_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bci-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_bald_ibis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oued_massa.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Oued_massa.JPG/220px-Oued_massa.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Oued_massa.JPG/330px-Oued_massa.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Oued_massa.JPG/440px-Oued_massa.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1002" data-file-height="649" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oued_massa.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The steppes around the Oued Massa are favoured feeding areas</div></div></div>
<p>This gregarious species <a href="/wiki/Commuting" title="Commuting">commutes</a> in flocks from the cliff breeding sites or winter roosts to its feeding areas, flying in a <a href="/wiki/V_formation" title="V formation">V formation</a>. The flocks may contain up to 100 birds in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_5-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> During the breeding season, the ibises regularly forage up to 15&#160;km (9.3&#160;mi) from the colony, and, although <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a> not in current cultivation is preferred for feeding, they will also use <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">fallow ground</a>, and occasionally even actively cultivated fields.<sup id="cite_ref-rspb2_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rspb2-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The northern bald ibis consumes a very wide variety of mainly animal food; <a href="/wiki/Faeces" class="mw-redirect" title="Faeces">faecal</a> analysis of the Moroccan breeding population has shown that lizards and <a href="/wiki/Darkling_beetle" title="Darkling beetle">tenebrionid beetles</a> predominate in the diet,<sup id="cite_ref-rspb2_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rspb2-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> although small mammals, ground-nesting birds, and invertebrates such as snails, scorpions, spiders, and caterpillars are also taken.<sup id="cite_ref-aewa_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aewa-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Males will sometimes "scrounge" food from females.<sup id="cite_ref-waza5_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-waza5-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> As the flock moves across the ground, the ibis uses its long bill to feel for food items in the loose, sandy soil. Since this bird hunts mainly by probing, a soft surface seems to be vital, and it is important that any vegetation is sparse, and not more than 15–20&#160;cm (6–8&#160;in) high.<sup id="cite_ref-aewa_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aewa-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_status">Conservation status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_bald_ibis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Conservation status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Birecikcolonynests.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Birecikcolonynests.JPG/220px-Birecikcolonynests.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Birecikcolonynests.JPG/330px-Birecikcolonynests.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Birecikcolonynests.JPG/440px-Birecikcolonynests.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Birecikcolonynests.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artificial nests at the <a href="/wiki/Birecik" title="Birecik">Birecik</a> colony</div></div></div>
<p>Although the northern bald ibis was long extinct in Europe, many colonies in Morocco and Algeria survived until the early 20th century, when they began to decline more rapidly, the last colony in Algeria disappearing in the late 1980s. In Morocco there were about 38 colonies in 1940 and 15 in 1975, but the last migratory populations in the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_Mountains" title="Atlas Mountains">Atlas Mountains</a> had vanished by 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-aewa_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aewa-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The species is <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered</a> according to the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">IUCN</a> scale, with an estimated population in 2018 of around 147 breeding pairs in the wild and over 1,000 in captivity. It was formerly considered <a href="/wiki/Critically_endangered" title="Critically endangered">critically endangered</a> until heavy conservation action secured the breeding sites in Morocco and even allowed the birds to expand to other sites, as well as the semi-wild population conserved in Turkey as well as the reintroduction projects in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aewa_43-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aewa-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The northern bald ibis is one of the key species to which the draft <i>Agreement on the Conservation of African-Eurasian Migratory Waterbirds</i> (<a href="/wiki/AEWA" class="mw-redirect" title="AEWA">AEWA</a>) applies, and it has a detailed, internationally agreed conservation action plan under the agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-aewa_43-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aewa-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> As a species that is threatened with extinction, it is listed on Appendix 1 of <a href="/wiki/CITES" title="CITES">CITES</a> (the Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora), which means that trade in wild-caught birds is illegal, and may be licensed only in exceptional circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-citesapp_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-citesapp-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-citestext_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-citestext-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The northern bald ibis has declined for several centuries, at least partly as a consequence of unidentified natural causes.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The more rapid decline in the past hundred years, with a loss of 98% of the population between 1900 and 2002,<sup id="cite_ref-serraabd_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-serraabd-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> is the result of a combination of factors. These include significant human persecution, especially hunting, and also the loss of steppe and non-intensive agricultural areas (particularly in Morocco), pesticide poisoning, disturbance, and dam construction.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The discovery in Jordan of three dead adults from the Turkish colony seemed to confirm that the overuse of pesticides is still a cause of death on migration. These birds were tracked by satellite after leaving Birecik; they stopped off briefly at the Syrian colony, and were later found dead in the Jordanian desert. Although the cause of death was initially thought to have been from poison, probably laid by chicken farmers to kill rodents, the autopsy revealed that they had actually been
electrocuted whilst standing on electricity pylons<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wild_populations">Wild populations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_bald_ibis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Wild populations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Morocco">Morocco</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_bald_ibis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Morocco">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Monitoring of Moroccan wild population is guaranteed by <a href="/wiki/BirdLife_International" title="BirdLife International">BirdLife International</a> partners, especially by <a href="/wiki/RSPB" class="mw-redirect" title="RSPB">RSPB</a>, <a href="/wiki/SEO/BirdLife" class="mw-redirect" title="SEO/BirdLife">SEO/BirdLife</a> and, recently <a href="/w/index.php?title=GREPOM&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="GREPOM (page does not exist)">GREPOM</a> in cooperation with <a href="/wiki/Souss-Massa_National_Park" title="Souss-Massa National Park">Souss-Massa National Park</a> administration<sup id="cite_ref-seo-marruecos_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seo-marruecos-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> and the support of institutions like <a href="/wiki/Prince_Albert_II_of_Monaco_Foundation" title="Prince Albert II of Monaco Foundation">Prince Albert II of Monaco Foundation</a><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> which is the Species Champion<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> for Northern Bald Ibis.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> For the first time in the species' recorded history, there is now evidence of population growth in the wild, and the population in Morocco increased to 100 breeding pairs in the decade prior to 2008<sup id="cite_ref-bci_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bci-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> and reached a record of 113 breeding pairs in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Simple site and species protection has facilitated this growth. Quantitative assessments of the importance of sites for breeding, roosting, and foraging have guided actions to prevent disturbance and the loss of key areas to mass tourism development. Wardening by members of the local community has reduced human intrusion and increased the perceived value of the birds. The provision of drinking water and the removal and deterrence of predators and competitors enhances breeding prospects, and monitoring has confirmed that <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a> and two-year <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">fallows</a> are key feeding habitats.<sup id="cite_ref-bci_39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bci-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> 
In early 2019 the total population in the two colonies of Souss-Massa National Park and Tamri reached 708 birds after 147 breeding pairs that laid eggs have produced 170 chicks in the last breeding season.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Maintaining such non-intensive land uses in the future may present major management challenges, and the recovery in the <a href="/wiki/Souss-Massa_National_Park" title="Souss-Massa National Park">Souss-Massa</a> region remains precarious because the population is concentrated in just a few places. However, it could provide opportunities for natural extension of the range to formerly occupied sites further north in Morocco.<sup id="cite_ref-bci_39-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bci-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Waldrapp_Ibis_is_feeding_a_chick.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Waldrapp_Ibis_is_feeding_a_chick.jpg/170px-Waldrapp_Ibis_is_feeding_a_chick.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Waldrapp_Ibis_is_feeding_a_chick.jpg/255px-Waldrapp_Ibis_is_feeding_a_chick.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Waldrapp_Ibis_is_feeding_a_chick.jpg/340px-Waldrapp_Ibis_is_feeding_a_chick.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1076" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Waldrapp_Ibis_is_feeding_a_chick.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Adult feeding a chick</div></div></div>
<p>The main cause of breeding failure at the Souss-Massa National Park is the loss of eggs to predators,<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> especially the <a href="/wiki/Common_raven" title="Common raven">common raven</a> which nest monitoring has shown to have had a serious impact at one sub-colony.<sup id="cite_ref-rspb2_23-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rspb2-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The effects of predators on adult birds have not been studied, but the very similar southern bald ibis, <i>Geronticus calvus</i>, is hunted by large <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">raptors</a>, particularly those that share its breeding cliffs.<sup id="cite_ref-Harrison_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> There is evidence of chick starvation in some years, but the main threats to breeding birds are human disturbance and the loss of feeding habitat.<sup id="cite_ref-rspb2_23-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rspb2-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> There was a dramatic mortality incident at the Moroccan colonies in May 1996, when 40 adults died or disappeared over a period of nine days. Although analyses have not identified the cause, an obscure virus, a toxin, or <a href="/wiki/Botulism" title="Botulism">botulism</a> are thought to be the most likely causes of the deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-rspb2_23-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rspb2-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Syria">Syria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_bald_ibis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Syria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Conservation efforts for the Northern bald ibis in Syria began with the discovery of an unreported relict colony of this species in early 2002 in the Palmyra desert.<sup id="cite_ref-serraabd_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-serraabd-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The bald ibises still breeding in Syria, discovered during an extensive biodiversity survey carried out as part of a FAO cooperation project, are the last living descendants of those depicted in Egyptian hieroglyphs from 4500 years ago. The discovery was made possible through the use of traditional ecological knowledge of the Bedouin nomads.<sup id="cite_ref-serraabd_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-serraabd-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the discovery in Syria, a successful community-based ibis breeding intensive protection program was established in Palmyra during years 2002–2004, in parallel with an extensive capacity building program in the benefit of the local community and staff from the Syrian Steppe Commission. Fourteen chicks successfully fledged during this period.<sup id="cite_ref-serra2009_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-serra2009-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beside protection and training operations, data on threats and on feeding and breeding ecology were collected in the field. An Ibis Protected Area was recommended and established, and an awareness and education program was also launched and successfully implemented.<sup id="cite_ref-serra2011_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-serra2011-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Two breeding failures were recorded in 2005 and 2008 following a change of project management and of ibis protection strategy, that occurred between 2004 and 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-serra2017_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-serra2017-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Three birds were tagged with satellite tags and the migratory route and wintering site of the colony were discovered in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-serra2009_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-serra2009-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Three surveys were undertaken at the wintering site on the Ethiopian highlands between 2006 and 2009, establishing that no immediate threats were present at the site.<sup id="cite_ref-serra2011_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-serra2011-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thanks to an IUCN project the Ibis Protected Area in Palmyra desert was further developed in 2008–2009, addressing the threats of infrastructure proliferation and oil company heavy prospection schemes.<sup id="cite_ref-serra2011_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-serra2011-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, it became apparent that only adults were reaching the wintering site in Ethiopia and that it was the low survival rate of immature birds - and thus an insufficient recruitment at the breeding colony in Palmyra - that was causing the slow and steady decline of the colony from 3 breeding pairs in 2002 to just 1 in 2010. Satellite tracking and surveys conducted in western Saudi Arabia during 2009–2010, with key cooperation of the Saudi Wildlife Authority, suggested that a combination of hunting and electrocution were causing a high mortality of dispersing immature ibises. This mortality is currently regarded as the main cause of the low recruitment occurred at the Palmyra colony during the years following the high breeding performance of period 2002-2004 (only 3 recruitment events out of 14 chicks fledged).<sup id="cite_ref-serra2015_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-serra2015-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A supplementation trial could be eventually conducted in 2010 by introducing captive-born chicks into the wild colony in Palmyra. For this aim, a first captive breeding center was established in Palmyra. Three chicks introduced at the wild colony in Palmyra followed a migrating wild adult for more than 1000&#160;km from Palmyra well into southwest Saudi Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-serra2017_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-serra2017-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The success of the trial, unique of its kind, reinvigorated the hopes that the colony could be still saved. Conservation efforts were interrupted in March 2011 due to the worsening of the political situation in Syria. Palmyra trained rangers have reportedly continued to protect the breeding birds even during the subsequent years. The last year a lone bird was seen returning to Palmyra is 2014 (it returned alone also in 2013). In 2015 no birds came back. As of 2017, some birds are still seen at the wintering grounds.<sup id="cite_ref-serra2017_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-serra2017-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Turkey">Turkey</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_bald_ibis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Turkey">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>With the loss of the genuinely wild Turkish population, the Ministry of Environment and Forestry's Directorate of Natural Preservation and National Parks established a new semi-wild colony at Birecik. This was heavily managed, with birds taken into captivity after the breeding season to prevent migration.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The program was successful, with numbers at 205 as of March 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-hurriyetdailynews.com_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hurriyetdailynews.com-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The intent is to allow the birds to migrate once the population reaches a stable 100 pairs, excluding young.<sup id="cite_ref-kelaynak_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kelaynak-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The birds are released in late January or early February to breed outside the cages on ledges and, mainly, in the nest boxes in the breeding station compound. The ibises are free flying and forage around the Birecik area in forest nurseries, agricultural fields, and along the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>, but supplementary food is also provided.<sup id="cite_ref-hurriyetdailynews.com_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hurriyetdailynews.com-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Following the end of the breeding season, the birds are taken into cages in late July or early August to prevent migration.<sup id="cite_ref-kelaynak_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kelaynak-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> A trial migration using tagged birds confirmed the risks presented to travelling birds by pesticides.<sup id="cite_ref-dead_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dead-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Later on the Syrian Civil War added one more reason to keep preventing migration.<sup id="cite_ref-hurriyetdailynews.com_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hurriyetdailynews.com-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reintroductions">Reintroductions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_bald_ibis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Reintroductions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hermit_Ibis_in_Vienna_Zoo_on_2013-05-14.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Hermit_Ibis_in_Vienna_Zoo_on_2013-05-14.png/220px-Hermit_Ibis_in_Vienna_Zoo_on_2013-05-14.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Hermit_Ibis_in_Vienna_Zoo_on_2013-05-14.png/330px-Hermit_Ibis_in_Vienna_Zoo_on_2013-05-14.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Hermit_Ibis_in_Vienna_Zoo_on_2013-05-14.png/440px-Hermit_Ibis_in_Vienna_Zoo_on_2013-05-14.png 2x" data-file-width="3136" data-file-height="3136" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hermit_Ibis_in_Vienna_Zoo_on_2013-05-14.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tiergarten_Sch%C3%B6nbrunn" title="Tiergarten Schönbrunn">Tiergarten Schönbrunn</a> in Vienna is running a successful breeding programme for reintroduction</div></div></div>
<p>The guidelines for the conservation and reintroduction of the northern bald ibis were established in 2003 at an International Advisory Group for Northern Bald Ibis (IAGNBI) conference in <a href="/wiki/Innsbruck" title="Innsbruck">Innsbruck</a> at the Alpenzoo, which maintains the European <a href="/wiki/Breed_registry" title="Breed registry">studbook</a> for the northern bald ibis.<sup id="cite_ref-eep_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eep-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IAGNBI2003_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IAGNBI2003-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Decisions taken at the meeting included:<sup id="cite_ref-IAGNBI2003_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IAGNBI2003-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>There should be no augmenting of the wild populations at <a href="/wiki/Souss-Massa_National_Park" title="Souss-Massa National Park">Souss-Massa</a> or in <a href="/wiki/Palmyra" title="Palmyra">Palmyra</a> using zoo-bred ibises.</li>
<li>There are two distinctive populations of northern bald ibis, and the separate ranges of the eastern and western forms should be respected.</li>
<li>In order to prepare birds for release, groups of chicks should be hand-reared by human "parents".</li>
<li>Migration routes and stop-over points will have to be taught to young birds, since it is unlikely that they will discover this information by themselves.</li></ul>
<p>A second conference in Spain in 2006 stressed the need to survey potential and former sites in north-west Africa and the Middle East for currently undetected colonies. The need to raise the standards of hygiene and husbandry in the <a href="/wiki/Birecik" title="Birecik">Birecik</a> aviaries was reiterated, and the prevalence of skin problems in a number of zoos reinforced the view that no zoo birds should be used for any free-flying trials.<sup id="cite_ref-IAGNBI2007_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IAGNBI2007-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> In future captive breeding and releasing programmes, only birds of known origin should be used.<sup id="cite_ref-Pegoraro_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pegoraro-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Zoo_populations">Zoo populations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_bald_ibis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Zoo populations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
